dear miss wordsworth i have just write the above endearing word when monkhouse tap I on the shoulder with a invitation to cold goose pye which i be not bird of that sort enough to decline mr m i be most happy to say be better mary have be torment with a rheumatism which be leave she i be suffer from the festivity of the season i wonder how my misuse carcase hold it out i have play have the experimental philosopher on it that be certain willy shall be welcome to a mince pye and a bout at commerce whenever he come he be in we eye i be glad you like my new year 's speculation everybody like they except the author of the pleasure of hope disappointment attend he how i like to be like and _ what i do _ to be like they flatter I in magazine newspaper and all the minor review the quarterly hold aloof but they must come into it in time or they leaf be waste paper salute trinity library in my name two special thing be worth see at cambridge a portrait of cromwell at sidney and a better of dr harvey -lrb- who find out that blood be red -rrb- at dr davy 's you should see they coleridge be pretty well i have not see he but hear often of he from alsop who send I hare and pheasant have charm for I i can not conjure up a kind similitude for those snowy flake -- let they keep to twelfth cake mr pari we cambridge friend have be in town you do not know the watford in trumpington street -- they be capital people ask any body you meet who be the biggest woman in cambridge -- and i 'll hold you a wager they 'll say mr smith she break down two bench in trinity garden one on the confines of st john 's which occasion a litigation between the society as to repair it in warm weather she retire into a ice-cellar -lrb- literally -rrb- and date the return of the year from a hot Thursday some 20 year back she sit in a room with opposite door and window to let in a thorough draught which give she slenderer friend tooth-ache she be to be see in the market every morning at 10 cheapen fowl which i observe the cambridge poulterer be not sufficiently careful to stump have now answer most of the point containd in you letter let I end with assure you of we very best kindness speech for food run thus : -- it hapn that day that _ gerismond _ the lawfull king of _ france _ banish by _ torismond _ who with a lustie crew of outlawe liue in that forrest that day in honor of he birth make a feast to all he bolde yeoman and frolickt it with store of wine and venison sit all at a long table vnder the shadow of limon tree : to that place by chance fortune conduct rosader who see such a crew of braue man haue store of that for want of which hee and adam perish hee sleep boldly to the boord end and salute the company thus -- whatsoeuer thou be that art maister of these lustie squire i salute thee as graciously as a man in extreame distresse may : knowe that i and a fellow friend of mine be here famish in the forrest for want of foode : perish we must vnlesse relieue by thy fauor therefore if thou be a gentleman giue meate to man and such as be euery way worthie of life : let the proudest squire that sit at thy table rise and encounter with I in any honorable point of activitie whatsoeuer and if he and thou proue I not a man send mee away comfortlesse : if thou refuse this as a niggard of thy cate i will haue amongst you with my sword for rather wil i die valiantly then perish with so cowardly a extreame -lrb- collier 's _ poetical decameron _ 174 eighth conversation -rrb- lamb compare with that the passage in as you like it ii 7 88 beginning with the follow account from _ chamber ' journal _ for September 27 1856 will show : -- about the latter part of the last century one zincke a artist of little note but grandson of the celebrated enameller of that name manufacture fictitious shakespeare by the score the most famous of zincke 's production be the well-known talma shakespeare which gentle charle lamb make a pilgrimage to pari to see and when he do see kneel down and kiss with idolatrous veneration zincke paint it on a larger panel than be necessary for the size of the picture and then cut away the superfluous wood so as to leave the remainder in the shape of a pair of bellows zincke probably be think of a muse of fire when he adopt this strange method of raise the wind but he make little by it for the dealer into whose hand the picture pass sell it as a curiosity not a original portrait for # 5 the buyer be a person of ingenuity and fonder of money than curiosity fabricate a series of letter to and from sir kenelm digby and pass over to france _ plant _ -- the slang term use among the less honest of the curiosity-dealing fraternity -- the picture and the letter in a old château near pari of course a confederate manage to discover the _ plant _ in the presence of witness and great be the excitement that ensue sir kenelm digby have be in france in the reign of charle i and the fictitious correspondence _ prove _ that the picture be a original and have be paint by queen elizabeth 's command on the lid of she favorite pair of bellows it really would seem that the more absurd a deception be the better it succeed all pari be in delight at possess a original shakespeare while the london amateur be in despair at such a treasure be lose to england the ingenious person soon find a purchaser and a high price recompense he for he trouble but more remain to be tell the happy purchaser take he treasure to ribet the first parisian picture-cleaner of the day to be clean ribet set to work but we may fancy he surprise as the superficial _ impasto _ of zincke wash off beneath the sponge and shakespeare become a female in a lofty headgear adorn with blue ribbon in a furious passion the purchaser run to the seller let we talk over the affair quietly say the latter i have be cheat as well as you : let we keep the matter secret if we let the public know it all pari and even london too will be laugh at we i will return you you money and take back the picture if you will employ ribet to restore it to the same condition as it be in when you receive it this fair proposition be accede to and ribet restore the picture but as he be a superior artist to zincke he greatly improve it and this improvement be attribute to he skill as a cleaner the secret be keep and the picture improve by clean be again in the market talma the great tragedian purchase it at even a higher price than that give by the first buyer talma value it highly enclose it in a case of morocco and gold and subsequently refuse 1000 napoleon for it and even when at last its whole history be disclose he still cherish it as a genuine memorial of the great bard by kind permission of mr bb macgeorge the owner both of the letter and bellows i be letter 339 charle lamb to bernard barton -lrb- January 9 1824 -rrb- dear bb -- do you know what it be to succumb under a insurmountable day mare -- a whoreson lethargy falstaff call it -- a indisposition to do any thing or to be any thing -- a total deadness and distaste -- a suspension of vitality -- a indifference to locality -- a numb soporifical goodfornothingness -- a ossification all over -- a oyster-like insensibility to the pass event -- a mind-stupor -- a brawny defiance to the needle of a thrusting-in conscience -- do you ever have a very bad cold with a total irresolution to submit to water gruel process -- this have be for many week my lot and my excuse -- my finger drag heavily over this paper and to my think it be three and twenty furlong from here to the end of this demi-sheet -- i have not a thing to say -- nothing be of more importance than another -- i be flatter than a denial or a pancake -- emptier than judge park 's wig when the head be in it -- duller than a country stage when the actor be off it -- a cypher -- a o -- i acknowledge life at all only by a occasional convulsional cough and a permanent phlegmatic pain in the chest -- i be weary of the world -- life be weary of I -- my day be go into twilight and i do not think it worth the expence of candle -- my wick have a thief in it but i can not muster courage to snuff it -- i inhale suffocation -- i can not distinguish veal from mutton -- nothing interest I -- ` tis 12 o'clock and thurtell be just now come out upon the new drop -- jack ketch alertly tuck up he greasy sleeve to do the last office of mortality yet can not i elicit a groan or a moral reflection -- if you tell I the world will be at end tomorrow i should just say will it -- i have not volition enough to dot my i 's -- much less to comb my eyebrow -- my eye be set in my head -- my brain be go out to see a poor relation in moorfield and they do not say when they would come back again -- my scull be a grub street attic to let -- not so much as a joint stool or a crackd jordan leave in it -- my hand write not i from habit as chicken run about a little when they head be off -- o for a vigorous fit of gout cholic tooth ache -- a earwig in my auditory a fly in my visual organ -- pain be life -- the sharper the more evidence of life -- but this apathy this death -- do you ever have a obstinate cold a six or seven week ' unintermitting chill and suspension of hope fear conscience and every thing -- yet do i try all i can to cure it i try wine and spirit and smoking and snuff in unsparing quantity but they all only seem to make I worse instead of better -- i sleep in a damp room but it do I no good i come home late o ' night but do not find any visible amendment who shall deliver I from the body of this death it be just 15 minute after 12 thurtell be by this time a good way on he journey bait at scorpion perhaps ketch be bargaining for he cast coat and waistcoat the jew demur at first at three coleridge esq my dear and honor friend unknown as you be in the true character either of you mind or of you heart to the greater part of you countryman and misrepresent as you work have be by those who have the ear of the vulgar it will seem wonderful to many that i should make choice of you from the circle of my friend to dedicate to you these beginning of my thought upon the most important subject of these or any time and when i state the reason to be that you have be more profitable to my faith in orthodox doctrine to my spiritual understanding of the word of god and to my right conception of the christian church than any or all of the man with whom i have entertain friendship and conversation it will perhaps still more astonish the mind and stagger the belief of those who have adopt as once i do myself the misrepresentation which be purchase for a hire and vend for a price concern you character and work you have only to shut you ear to what they ignorantly say of you and earnestly to meditate the deep thought with which you be instinct and give they a suitable body and form that they may live then silently commit they to the good sense of age yet to come in order to be rank hereafter amongst the most gifted sage and greatest benefactor of you country enjoy and occupy the quiet which after many trial the providence of god have bestow upon you in the bosom of you friend and may you be spare until you have make know the multitude of you thought unto those who at present value or shall hereafter arise to value they worth i have partake so much high intellectual enjoyment from be admit into the close and familiar intercourse with which you have honor I and you many conversation concern the revelation of the christian faith have be so profitable to I in every sense as a student and a preacher of the gospel as a spiritual man and a christian pastor and you high intelligence and great learning have at all time so kindly stoop to my ignorance and inexperience that not merely with the affection of friend to friend and the honor due from youth to experienced age but with the gratitude of a disciple to a wise and generous teacher of a anxious inquirer to the good man who have help he in the way of truth i do now presume to offer you the first-fruit of my mind since it receive a new impulse towards truth and a new insight into its depths from listen to you discourse accept they in good part and be assure that however insignificant in themselves they be the offering of a heart which love you heart and of a mind which look up with reverence to you mind edward irving old friend thornton be leigh hunt 's son thornton leigh hunt whom lamb have address in verse in 1815 as my favorite child he be now fourteen -rrb- letter 358 charle where nature place it -rrb- by a dark blue petticoat of a still thicker texture so that it hang in large plait where it be gather in behind over this in front be tie tightly round the waist so as to keep all trim and compact a dark apron the string of which pass over the little fulled skirt of the jacket behind and make it stick out smartly and tastily while it clip the waist in the head-gear consist of a sort of mob cap nothing of which but the edge round the face can be see on account of the kerchief -lrb- of flowered cotton -rrb- which be pass over it hood fashion and half tie under the chin this head-kerchief be in place of the bonnet -- a thing not to be see among the whole five hundred female who make up this pleasant show indeed vary the color of the different article this description apply to every dress of the whole assembly except that in some the fineness of the day have dispense with the kerchief and leave the snow-white cap expose and in other the whole figure a copy for you and will you accept it shall i _ lend _ you at the same time my sole copy of the former volume -lrb- oh return it -rrb- for a month or two in return you shall favor I with the loan of one of those norfolk-bred grunter that you laud so highly i promise not to keep it above a day what a funny name bungay be i never dream of a correspondent thence i use to think of it as some utopian town or borough in gotham land i now believe in its existence as part of merry england -lrb- _ some line scratch out _ -rrb- the part i have scratch out be the best of the letter let I have you dear mr dyer -- i be very uneasy about a _ book _ which i either have lose or leave at you house on Thursday it be the book i go out to fetch from miss buffam 's while the tripe be fry it be call phillip 's theatrum poetarum but it be a english book i think i leave it in the parlor it be mr cary 's book and i would not lose it for the world pray if you find it book it at the swan snow hill by a edmonton stage immediately direct to mr lamb church-street edmonton or write to say you can not find it i be quite anxious about it if it be lose i shall never like tripe again with kindest love to mr dyer and all yours truly c lamb -lrb- in the life of hf cary by he son we read : he -lrb- lamb -rrb- have borrow of my father phillip 's _ theatrum poetarum anglicanorum _ which be return by lamb 's friend mr moxon with the leaf fold down at the account of sir philip sydney mr cary acknowledge the receipt of the book by the follow line to the memory of charle lamb so should it be my gentle friend thy leaf last close at sydney 's end thou too like sydney wouldst have give the water thirsting and near heaven nay be it wine fill have to the brim thou hadst look would hard but give like he and art thou mingle then among those famous son of ancient song and do they gather round and praise thy relish of they nobler lay wax in mirth to hear thee tell with what strange mortal thou didst dwell at thy quaint sally more delighted than any 's long among they light ` tis do : and thou hast join would a crew to whom thy soul be justly due and yet i think where'er thou be they 'll scarcely love thee more than we this be the last letter of charle lamb who trip and fall in church street edmonton on December 22 and die of erysipelas on December 27 at the time of he death lamb be very nearly sixty he birthday be February 10 mary lamb with occasional lapse into sound health survive he until may 20 1847 at first she continue to live at edmonton but a few year later move to the house of mr parson sister of she old nurse miss jame in st john 's wood i append three letter two write and one inspire by she to miss jane norri one of the daughter of randal norri of the friend mention 41 alpha road regent 's park -rrb- christmas day -lrb- 1841 -rrb- my dear jane -- many thanks for you kind present -- you michalma goose i think mr moxon have write to thank you -- the turkey and nice apple come yesterday give my love to you dear mother i be unhappy to find you note in the basket for i be always think of you all and wonder when i shall ever see any of you again i long to shew you what a nice snug place i have get into -- in the midst of a pleasant little garden i have a room for myself and my old book on the ground floor and a little bedroom up two pair of stair when you come to town if you have not time to go -lrb- to -rrb- the moxon a omnibus from the bell and crown in holborn would -lrb- bring -rrb- you to we door in -lrb- a -rrb- quarter of a hour if you dear mother do not venture so far i will contrive to pop down to see -lrb- she -rrb- love and all seasonable wish to you sister and mary & c i be in the midst of many friend -- mr & mrs kenney mr & mrs hood bar -lrb- r -rrb- on field & he brother frank & they wife & c all within a short walk if the lodger be go i shall have a bedroom will hold two heaven bless & preserve you all in health and happiness many a long year yours affectionately ma lamb letter 616 mary lamb to jane norri oct 3 1842 my dear jane norri -- thanks many thanks my dear friend for you kind remembrance what a nice goose that and all alpha road regent 's park london July 25 1843 madam -- miss lamb have see the death of you dear mother in the time news paper be most anxious to hear from or to see one of you as she wish to know how you intend settle yourselve and to have a full account of you dear mother 's last illness she be much shock on reading of she death and appear very vex that she have not be to see she -lrb- and -rrb- want very much to come down and see you both but we be really afraid to let she take the journey if either of you be come up to town she would be glad if you would call upon she but should you not be likely to come soon she would be very much pleased if one of you would have the goodness to write a few line to she as she be most anxious about you she beg you to excuse she write to you herself as she do not feel equal to it she ask I yesterday to write for she i be happy to say she be at present pretty well although you dear mother 's death appear to dwell much upon she mind she desire she kindest love to you both and hope to hear from you very soon if you be equal to write i sincerely hope you will oblige she and be madam you obedient & c sarah jame pray do not invite she to come down car and steed of fire : to I as glorious seem the change accord to thy worth as instantaneous and as strange thy exit from this earth something which wake a deeper thrill these few brief word unfold letter 469 _ page _ 846 -rrb- we gentle charle have pass would away from earth 's short bondage free and leave to we its leaden day and mist-enshrouded sea here by the restless ocean 's side sweet hour of hope have fly when first the triumph of its tide seem would omen of we own that eager joy the sea-breeze give when first it raise he hair sink with each day 's retire wave beyond the reach of prayer the sun-blink that through drizzle mist to flicker hope akin lone wave with feeble fondness kiss would no smile as faint can win yet not in vain with radiance weak the heavenly stranger gleam -- not of the world it light to speak but that from whence it stream that world we patient sufferer seek serene with pity eye as if he mount spirit catch the wisdom of the sky with boundless love it look would abroad for one bright moment give shone with a loveliness that aw would and quiver would into heaven a year make slow by care and toil have pace its weary round since death enrich would with kindred spoil the snow-clad frost-ribb would grind then lamb with whose endearing name we boy we proudly grace shrink from the warmth of sweeter fame than mightier bard embrace still 'twa a mournful joy to think we darling might supply for year to we a living link to name that can not die and though such fancy gleam no more on earthly sorrow 's night truth 's nobler torch unveil the shore which lend to both its light the nurseling there that hand may take none ever grasp would in vain and smile of well-known sweetness wake without they tinge of pain though ` twixt the child and child-like bard late seem distinction wide they now may trace in heaven 's regard how near they be ally within the infant 's ample brow blythe fancy lay unfurl would which all uncrush 'd may open now to charm a sinless world though the soft spirit of those eye might ne'er with lamb 's compete -- ne'er sparkle with a wit as wise or melt in tear as sweet that calm and unforgotten look a kindred love reveal with he who never friend forsake or hurt a thing that feel in think profound in wildest glee in sorrow dark and strange the soul of lamb 's bright infancy endure no spot or change from trait of each we love receive for comfort nobler scope while light which child-like genius leave confirm the infant 's hope and in that hope with sweetness fraught be ache heart beguile to blend in one delightful think the poet and the child edward fitzgerald 's the meadow in spring from hone 's _ year book _ -lrb- _ see letter _ 535 _ page _ 938 -rrb- ` tis a sad sight to see the year die when autumn 's last wind set the yellow wood sigh sigh oh sigh when such a time cometh i do retire into a old room beside a bright fire oh pile a bright fire and there i sit read old thing of knight and lady while the wind sing : oh drearily sing i never look out nor attend to the blast for all to be see be the leaf fall fast : fall fall but close at the hearth like a cricket sit i reading of summer and chivalry : gallant chivalry then with a old friend i talk of we youth how 'twa gladsome but often foolish forsooth but gladsome gladsome or to get merry we sing a old rhyme that make the wood ring again in summer time : sweet summer time then take we to smoking silent and snug : naught pass between we save a brown jug sometimes sometimes and sometimes a tear will rise in each eye see the two old friend so merrily so merrily and ere to bed go we go we down by the ash we kneel on the knee pray pray thus then live i till break the gloom of winter the bold sun be with I in the room shine shine then the cloud part swallow soar between : the spring be awake and the meadow be green -- i jump up like mad break the old pipe in twain and away to the meadow the meadow again epsilon jame montgomery 's the common lot -lrb- _ see letter _ 535 _ page _ 938 -rrb- a birth-day meditation during a solitary winter walk of seven mile between a village in derbyshire and sheffield when the ground be cover with snow the sky serene and the morning air intensely pure once in the flight of age past there live a man : -- and who be he -- mortal howe'er thy lot be cast that man resemble thee unknown the region of he birth the land in which he die unknown : he name have perish would from the earth this truth survive alone : -- that joy and grief and hope and fear alternate triumph would in he breast he bliss and woe -- a smile a tear -- oblivion hide the rest the bound pulse the languid limb the change spirit ' rise and fall we know that these be feel by he for these be feel by all he suffer would -- but he pang be o'er enjoy would -- but he delight be flee have friend -- he friend be now no more and foe -- he foe be dead he love -- but whom he love the grave have lose in its unconscious womb : o she be fair -- but nought could save she beauty from the tomb he see whatever thou hast see encounter would all that trouble thee : he be -- whatever thou hast be he be -- what thou shalt be the rolling season day and night sun moon and star the earth and main erewhile he portion life and light to he exist in vain the cloud and sunbeam o'er he eye that once they shades and glory throw have leave in yonder silent sky no vestige where they fly the annals of the human race they ruin since the world begin of he afford no other trace than this -- there live a man November 4 1805 barry cornwall 's epistle to charle lamb on he emancipation from clerkship -lrb- write over a flask of sherri -rrb- from _ english song _ -lrb- _ see letter _ 551 _ page _ 952 -rrb- dear lamb i drink to thee -- to _ thee _ marry to sweet liberty what old friend and art thou free from the bondage of the pen free from care and toil indeed free to wander amongst man when and howsoe'er thou wilt _ all _ thy drop of labor spill on those huge and figure page which will sleep unclasp would for age little know who do wield the quill that traverse they white field come -- another mighty health thou hast earn would thy sum of wealth -- countless ease -- immortal leisure -- day and night of boundless pleasure checquer would by no dream of pain such as hang on clerk-like brain like a night-mare and doth press the happy soul from happiness oh happy thou -- whose all of time -lrb- day and eve and morning prime -rrb- be fill would with talk on pleasant theme -- or vision quaint which come in dream such as panther would bacchus rule when he rod be on the school mix wisdom with they wine -- or perhaps thy wit so fine strayeth in some elder book whereon we modern solon look with severe ungifted eye wonder what thou seest to prize happy thou whose skill can take pleasure at each turn and slake thy thirst by every fountain 's brink where less wise man would pause to shrink : sometimes ` mid stately avenue with cowley thou or marvel 's muse dost walk or gray by eton 's tower or pope in hampton 's chesnut bower or walton by he loved lea stream : or dost thou with we milton dream of eden and the apocalypse and hear the word from he great lip speak -- in what grove or hazel shade for muse meditation make dost wander -- or on penshurst lawn where sidney 's fame have time to dawn and die ere yet the hate of man could envy at he perfect pen or dost thou in some london street -lrb- with voice fill would and throng foot -rrb- loiter with mien ` twixt grave and gay -- or take along some pathway sweet thy calm suburban way happy beyond that man of ross whom mere content could ne'er engross art thou -- with hope health learn leisure friend book thy thought a endless pleasure -- yet -- yet -- -lrb- for when be pleasure make sunshine all without a shade -rrb- thou perhaps as now thou rovest through the busy scene thou lovest with a idler 's careless look turn some moth-pierced book feel ` st a sharp and sudden woe for vision vanish long ago and then thou think ` st how time have flee over thy unsilvered head snatch many a fellow mind away and leave -- what -- behind nought alas save joy and pain mingled ever like a strain of music where the discord vie with the truer harmony so perhaps with thee the vein be sully ever -- so the chain of habit and affection old like a weight of solid gold presseth on thy gentle breast till sorrow rob thee of thy rest ay : so ' t must be -- ev 'n i -lrb- whose lot the fairy love so long forget -rrb- seat beside this sherri wine and near to book and shape divine which poet and the painter past have work in line that aye shall last -- ev 'n i with shakspeare 's self beside I and one whose tender talk can guide I through fear and pain and troublous theme whose smile doth fall upon my dream like sunshine on a stormy sea -- want _ something _ -- when i think of thee list of letter alphabetically arrange ader
